والشيخ محمد محي الدين لم ينس أنْ يهتم بالنَّاشئة والجيل الجديد، وهو أول من فكر في تأليف كتب دينية مزدانة بما يناسب الأطفال، فألف خمسة كتب أجزاء اثنين للبنين ، واثنين للإناث، وكتابا مشتركا ، وقد ذاعت هذه الكتب آنذاك ، حتى كان المرحوم الدكتور عبد الوهاب عزام يذكر أنه شاهدها مترجمة بالفارسية والتركية.
نموذج من ثناء ومدح شيوخه له:
لما ألَّفَ الشيخ محمد محي الدين شرحه الماتع على «المسايرة» وكان في تلك الفترة مدرسا في القسم الثانوي في الأزهر الشريف، عرض شرحه على شيخه العلامة عبد المجيد اللبان، فكتب له بعد الديباجة: (ولدي العزيز الشيخ محمد الدين عبد الحميد! أهديك تحياتي ، وأُعلمك بأني قرير العين بما صنعت، قرأتُ كتابك: شرح المسايرة للكمال ابن الهمام» فأعجبني أسلوبه ، وسترني جزالته ، جمع من التوحيد ما جاء في كتاب الله فهو من الهدي وخير الهدي هداه خلا مِن التعقيد، وسما عن الابتذال، فكان سهلا ممتعا، بحر علم يُصحح عقيدة من قرأه، ومنهل تهذيب يثقف
Biography of Muhammad Muhyi al-Din Abd al-Hamid. 2 - Among his important works related to the subject of this our book: his writing of a delightful, well-edited commentary on al-Musayara in the science of kalam, by Imam Kamal ibn al-Humam. 3 - A gloss on Ithaaf al-Mureed, which he named al-Nizam al-Farid bi-Tahqiq Jawharat al-Tawhid, and it is included in this our book. 4 - And he also has: 'The Personal Status in Islamic Law'. 5 - And al-Tuhfa al-Saniyya bi-Sharh al-Muqaddima al-Ajurrumiyya. 6 - And a treatise on the etiquette of research and debate. - And 'Tasrif al-Af'al'. 8 - And he has a delightful gloss on Qatr al-Nada wa-Ball al-Sada. 9 - And a gloss on Sharh Shudhur al-Dhahab. 10 - And on Awḍah al-Masalik by Ibn Hisham in four volumes. 11 - And he has a commentary on the book al-Insaf fi Masa'il al-Khilaf bayn al-Basriyyin wa-al-Kufiyyin by Ibn al-Anbari. 12 - And he has an important gloss on Sharh al-Ushmuni in four volumes. 13 - And a commentary on al-Mufassal by al-Zamakhshari. 14 - And he has the book 'Tanqih al-Azhariyya'. This, and he has very great efforts in publishing heritage books, achieving them with superior, excellent editing, with great care in correcting the text, controlling the problematic, and explaining the obscure, in various arts and sciences, including, by way of example not limitation: Sharh Shafiyat Ibn al-Hajib, al-Mukhtar min al-Sihah, Adab al-Katib wa-al-Sha'ir by Ibn al-Athir, Nahj al-Balagha by al-Sharif al-Radi, al-Muwazana bayn Abi Tammam wa-al-Buhturi by al-Amidi, Wafa' al-Wafa bi-Akhbar Dar al-Mustafa by al-Samhudi, and from them Tawdih al-Afkar li-Ma'ani Tanqih al-Anzar by al-San'ani, al-Farq bayn al-Firaq by al-Baghdadi, Maqalat al-Islamiyyin by Imam al-Ash'ari, al-Targhib wa-al-Tarhib by al-Mundhiri, and other books of the treasures of heritage. And Shaykh Muhammad Muhyi al-Din did not forget to care for the youth and the new generation; he was the first to think of writing religious books adorned with what is suitable for children. So he wrote five books: two parts for boys, two for girls, and one joint book. These books became widespread at that time, so much so that the late Dr. Abd al-Wahhab Azzam mentioned that he saw them translated into Persian and Turkish. A sample of the praise and commendation of his teachers for him: When Shaykh Muhammad Muhyi al-Din wrote his delightful commentary on al-Musayara, and he was at that time a teacher in the secondary section of al-Azhar al-Sharif, he presented his commentary to his teacher, the scholar Abd al-Majid al-Laban, who wrote to him after the preamble: 'My dear son Shaykh Muhammad Muhyi al-Din Abd al-Hamid! I send you my greetings, and I inform you that I am delighted with what you have done. I read your book: Sharh al-Musayara li-l-Kamal Ibn al-Humam, and I was impressed by its style, and its excellence covered me. It gathered from tawhid what came in the Book of Allah, so it is from guidance, and the best guidance is his guidance. It is free from complexity, and rises above triviality, so it is easy and delightful, a sea of knowledge that corrects the creed of whoever reads it, and a fountain of refinement that cultivates.'
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of the scholar Muhammad Muhyi al-Din Abd al-Hamid, focusing on his scholarly output and achievements. It begins by listing his important works, many of which are commentaries and glosses on classical texts in various fields: kalam (theology), grammar, jurisprudence, and language. The list includes a commentary on al-Musayara in kalam, a gloss on Ithaaf al-Mureed (which is the very book we are studying), and works on Islamic personal status law, Arabic grammar (e.g., al-Ajurrumiyya, Qatr al-Nada), and research methodology. It then highlights his monumental efforts in editing and publishing heritage books, providing examples of key texts he worked on, such as commentaries on Ibn al-Hajib, al-Sihah, and classical works by Ibn al-Athir, al-Sharif al-Radi, al-Amidi, al-Samhudi, al-San'ani, al-Baghdadi, al-Ash'ari, and al-Mundhiri. The passage notes his pioneering role in writing simplified religious books for children, with five volumes for boys and girls. Finally, it presents a concrete example of recognition from his teacher, Abd al-Majid al-Laban, who praised his commentary on al-Musayara for its clarity, depth, and adherence to Quranic guidance, emphasizing that it avoids complexity and vulgarity while being intellectually stimulating and spiritually uplifting.

The Scholarly Legacy of Muhammad Muhyi al-Din Abd al-Hamid
Biography of the editor, his works, praise, and death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scholarly credentials and contributions of the editor of this text, Muhammad Muhyi al-Din Abd al-Hamid.

Core Idea
A scholar who mastered multiple sciences and pioneered religious children's literature.

Before delving into the theological content of the book, it is important to know the man who edited it. This biography shows his vast knowledge, his innovation in writing for children, and the high praise he received from his teachers. Understanding his background helps us trust the quality of the edition.

Guided Path
1
Read the biography carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation once. Note that this is a biography of the editor, not the author of the main text.
Pay attention to the list of works and the two pieces of praise.
2
Identify his scholarly works
The passage lists several books he published. These show his expertise in various fields: grammar, literature, history, theology, and more.
He also wrote children's books on religion, an innovation.
3
Analyze the praise from his teachers
Abd al-Majid al-Laban praises his commentary on 'al-Musayarah' for its clarity and doctrinal correctness. Muhammad Ali al-Najjar compares him to al-Tabari, saying he masters grammar, jurisprudence, hadith, and kalam.
Both teachers emphasize his breadth and depth.
4
Conclude with his character and death
The passage notes his intelligence, good conduct, and dedication to al-Azhar. His death date is given. This reinforces his reliability.
He died in 1972 at over seventy years of age.

And these books spread at that time, so much so that the late Dr. Abd al-Wahhab Azzam mentioned that he saw them translated into Persian and Turkish. A sample of the praise and commendation of his teachers for him: When Shaykh Muhammad Muhyi al-Din composed his delightful commentary on 'al-Musayarah' and was at that time a teacher in the secondary section at al-Azhar al-Sharif, he presented his commentary to his teacher the scholar Abd al-Majid al-Laban, who wrote to him after the preamble: 'My dear son, Shaykh Muhammad al-Din Abd al-Hamid! I send you my greetings, and inform you that I am delighted with what you have done. I read your book: Sharh al-Musayarah li al-Kamal Ibn al-Humann. I was impressed by its style, and its richness amazed me. It gathered from Tawhid what came in the Book of Allah, so it is from guidance, and the best guidance is its guidance, free from complexity and above commonness. It was easy and enjoyable, a sea of knowledge that corrects the creed of whoever reads it, and a fountain of refinement that cultivates the soul of whoever remembers it with a sound heart. May Allah benefit by you, accept your work, and reward you abundantly. Accept the praise of your affectionate father, admiring your talents: Abd al-Majid al-Laban. Muharram 1348 AH, corresponding to June 1929.' The statement of the scholar Muhammad Ali al-Najjar about him: And Shaykh Muhammad Ali al-Najjar, member of the Arabic Language Academy, said about him: 'It was said of al-Tabari: He was like a Qur'an reciter who knows only the Qur'an, and a hadith scholar who knows only hadith, and a jurist who knows only jurisprudence, and a grammarian who knows only grammar, and an arithmetician who knows only arithmetic. Likewise, it is said of Shaykh Muhyi al-Din: He is like a grammarian who knows only grammar, and a jurist who knows only jurisprudence, and a hadith scholar who knows only hadith, and a theologian who knows only kalam. And the proof of that is what he composed and published of books in these arts.' His death: In brief, he was a marvel in intelligence and cleverness, and good conduct, and zeal for al-Azhar, its history and its men. He was also known for moderation in speech, guarding himself, and controlling his behavior. He continued on that path until Allah the Exalted took his soul on (25) of Dhu al-Qa'dah of the year (1392 AH), corresponding to (30) December of the year (1972 CE), at over seventy years of age. May Allah have mercy on him, elevate his rank, honor his abode, and reward him well for Islam and the Muslims. (1) (1) Sources of the biography: The book 'al-Nahdah al-Islamiyyah fi Siyar A'lamiha al-Mu'asirin' by Dr. Ibrahim Rajab al-Bayyumi, and 'Madkhal ila Tarikh Nashr al-Turath al-Arabi' by the verifier professor Dr. Mahmud al-Tanahi, and the book 'Jumharat A'lam al-Azhar al-Sharif fi al-Qarnayn al-Rabi' Ashar wa al-Khamis Ashar al-Hijriyyayn' by Shaykh Dr. Usamah al-Sayyid al-Azhari.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of the editor, Muhammad Muhyi al-Din Abd al-Hamid. It begins by listing some of his scholarly works across various disciplines, such as commentaries, literary works, and historical texts, establishing his expertise in multiple fields. It then highlights his unique contribution to writing religious books for children, an innovative idea that gained international recognition. The core of the passage presents two pieces of praise from his teachers. The first is a detailed letter from his teacher Abd al-Majid al-Laban, who commends his commentary on 'al-Musayarah' for its clarity, depth, and doctrinal correctness, calling it a sea of knowledge that corrects creed. The second praise is from Muhammad Ali al-Najjar, who compares him to al-Tabari in his mastery of multiple sciences, specifically stating that he excels in grammar, jurisprudence, hadith, and kalam (theology), as evidenced by his published works. The passage concludes with an account of his character and death, noting his intelligence, modesty, and dedication to al-Azhar, and provides the date of his death in both Hijri and Gregorian calendars, along with a prayer for mercy. A footnote lists the sources from which the biography is drawn. The overall purpose is to establish the editor's scholarly authority and virtuous character, lending credibility to his editorial work on the present book.

Eulogy of the Editor's Multiscience Expertise
A eulogy comparing the editor's scholarly breadth to al-Tabari

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muhammad Ali al-Najjar praised the editor by comparing his expertise to that of al-Tabari.

Core Idea
Muhammad Ali al-Najjar compares the editor's mastery to al-Tabari's legendary breadth.

This passage is a eulogy from the scholar Muhammad Ali al-Najjar, who lauds the editor's mastery across multiple Islamic sciences. He draws a parallel to the great scholar al-Tabari, about whom it was said he mastered each science so completely that one would think he only knew that science. This rhetorical device underscores the editor's exceptional versatility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the eulogy's flow and content.
Notice the repeated structure: 'like a ... who knows only ...'
2
Identify the key figure being praised
Recognize that the passage is about Shaykh Muhyi al-Din (the editor), and it compares him to al-Tabari, a renowned scholar of the past.
Al-Tabari is known for his tafsir and history, but here his multifield expertise is highlighted.
3
List the sciences mentioned
Make a list of the Islamic sciences: grammar (nahw), fiqh, hadith, kalam (theology). For al-Tabari, also qira'at and mathematics.
This illustrates the breadth of their knowledge.
4
Understand the rhetorical device
The phrase 'like a ... who knows only that science' is hyperbole to emphasize deep specialization. It does not mean they actually knew nothing else.
This is a common praise format in classical biographies.
5
Reflect on the significance
Consider why such a comparison elevates the editor: it places him among the most versatile scholars in Islamic tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلمة العلامة محمد علي النجار فيه
وقال عنه الشيخ محمد علي النجار عضو مجمع اللغة العربية: (لقد قيل في الطبري: إنه كان كالقارئ الذي لا يعرف إلا القرآن ، وكالمحدث الذي لا يعرف إلا الحديث، وكالفقيه الذي لا يعرف إلا الفقه، وكالنحوي الذي لا يعرف إلا النحو، وكالحاسب الذي لا يعرف إلا الحساب، وكذا يُقال في الشيخ محي الدين إنه كالنحوي الذي لا يعرف إلا النحو، وكالفقيه الذي لا يعرف إلا الفقه والمحدث الذي لا يعرف إلا الحديث ، وكالمتكلم الذي لا يعرف إلا الكلام، وآية ذلك ما ألّفه وأخرجه من الكتب في هذه الفنون).
The statement of the scholar Muhammad Ali al-Najjar about him: And the Shaykh Muhammad Ali al-Najjar, member of the Arabic Language Academy, said: 'It was said about al-Tabari: He was like a reciter who knows only the Quran, and like a muhaddith who knows only hadith, and like a faqih who knows only fiqh, and like a grammarian who knows only grammar, and like a mathematician who knows only mathematics. Likewise, it is said about Shaykh Muhyi al-Din: He is like a grammarian who knows only grammar, and like a faqih who knows only fiqh, and like a muhaddith who knows only hadith, and like a mutakallim who knows only kalam. And proof of that is what he authored and published of books in these arts.'
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is a eulogy for the editor, Shaykh Muhyi al-Din, by the scholar Muhammad Ali al-Najjar. It begins by drawing a parallel to the famous scholar al-Tabari, about whom it was said he mastered each Islamic science to the point of being known only for that science. The eulogy then applies this to Shaykh Muhyi al-Din, listing his expertise in grammar, fiqh, hadith, and kalam, implying he is equally proficient in each. The passage concludes by pointing to his authored works as evidence of his breadth. The structure follows a parallelism: what was said about al-Tabari is said about the editor.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the passage means the editor only knew one subject (e.g., just grammar) and was ignorant of others.
Correction: The phrase 'like a grammarian who knows only grammar' is a rhetorical hyperbole to emphasize deep specialization; it actually indicates mastery of multiple sciences, not limitation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The eulogy compares the editor to al-Tabari, who could be mistaken for knowing only one science. In your own life, is there a single area of Islamic knowledge you have mastered so deeply that others associate you with it? If not, why not? What step will you take this week to change that?
?
The proof of the editor's expertise is his authored works. If your knowledge were to be judged by what you have produced, what would be your evidence? Write down one concrete output you can create in the next month to substantiate your learning.
?
The editor is called a 'mutakallim'—a master of kalam. Have you ever invested serious effort in understanding the proofs of Islamic theology, or do you rely on others? What specific theological argument from this course will you memorize and internalize before the next lesson?

Biographical note on the editor's character and death.

Core Idea
A life of intelligence, moderation, and devotion to al-Azhar.

This lesson presents a brief biographical sketch of the editor, Shaykh Muhyi al-Din. It highlights his intellectual gifts, his dignified conduct, and his unwavering commitment to al-Azhar. As we read, we will see how his character earned him a lasting prayer for mercy and reward.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

Identify the key character traits

Life of Imam al-Laqqani
beginner
3 min
The Author's Humble Self-Introduction
Al-Laqani introduces himself with humble epithets and his full name.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Laqani introduces himself with deep humility and why this style is significant in Islamic scholarly tradition.

Core Idea
Humility is the first credential of a scholar.

Al-Laqani opens his work not with self-praise but with self-abasement, calling himself a poor, lowly, perishing slave. This sets the tone for the entire text: knowledge is a gift from Allah, not a personal achievement. Let's examine his words carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the self-introduction
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the sequence of epithets and the author's name.
2
Understand the epithets
Identify each adjective: 'al-ʿabd' (the slave), 'al-faqīr' (the poor), 'al-ḥaqīr' (the lowly), 'al-fānī' (the perishing). Reflect on what each emphasizes about human dependence on Allah.
These are not mere formalities; they carry deep theological meaning.
3
Reflect on scholarly humility
Consider why a scholar would begin a theological work with such humility. What does it teach about the proper attitude for seeking knowledge?
This connects to the broader Islamic emphasis on sincerity and reliance on Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد فيقول العبد الفقير الحقير (۲) الفاني (۳) ، عبد السلام بن إبراهيم المالكي (٤) اللقاني، ستر الله عيوبه ، وغفر ذنوبَهُ: قد كنتُ لخصت (٥) ما علقه أستاذنا (٦) من عُمْدَةِ المُرِيدِ» على عقيدته
And after: So says the slave, the poor, the lowly, the perishing, ʿAbd al-Salām ibn Ibrāhīm al-Mālikī al-Laqānī—may Allah cover his faults and forgive his sins—: I have indeed summarized what our teacher (ustādhunā) annotated from 'ʿUmdat al-Murīd' upon his creed.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is the author's self-introduction and statement of purpose. After the opening praises (implied by 'wa baʿd'), the author refers to himself using a series of humble epithets: 'al-ʿabd al-faqīr al-ḥaqīr al-fānī' (the slave, the poor, the lowly, the perishing). He then gives his full name: ʿAbd al-Salām ibn Ibrāhīm al-Mālikī al-Laqānī, followed by a prayer for forgiveness. Next he declares that he has made a summary (lakhkhaṣtu) of what 'our teacher' (ustādhunā) wrote as annotations (taʿlīq) from the work 'ʿUmdat al-Murīd' on his creed (ʿaqīdah). 'Ustādhunā' here refers to his father (as the footnote explains), so the author is presenting his work as a digest of his father's commentary on the original poem (Jawharat al-Tawḥīd). The phrase 'min ʿumdat al-murīd ʿalā ʿaqīdatihi' indicates the source: the book 'ʿUmdat al-Murīd' which is a commentary on the author's own creedal poem. This establishes the work as a filial and scholarly transmission.

Key Terms
العبد الفقير الحقير
اللقاني
ستر الله عيوبه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the author is being overly self-deprecating or that it's just a routine formula.
Correction: The epithets are carefully chosen to express the Islamic teaching that humans are entirely dependent on Allah, especially when engaging in theology. It is a conscious act of worship and humility.

Checkpoint
What does the author call himself in the opening?
A) The wise and learned
B) The poor and lowly slave
C) The honored master
D) The proud scholar

Key Takeaway
Al-Laqani's humble self-introduction grounds the entire work in a posture of dependence on Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you were to introduce yourself as Al-Laqani does—as a slave, poor, lowly, perishing—how would that change the way you see yourself right now? Would you still cling to your titles and achievements?
?
Think of a time you felt proud of your knowledge or status. How would Al-Laqani's prayer 'may Allah cover his faults' reshape that moment? What faults are you hiding that you haven't asked Allah to cover?
?
Al-Laqani could have listed his scholarly credentials first. Instead, he led with utter dependence. When you introduce yourself—at work, among friends, even in your own mind—what comes first: your accomplishments or your need for Allah? What would it cost you to reverse that order starting today?

The Author's Purpose: Summarizing His Father's Commentary
Al-Laqani states he summarized his teacher's annotations from 'Umdat al-Murīd' on his creed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Laqani's purpose in writing this text: to summarize his father's annotations for the benefit of students.

Core Idea
Knowledge is transmitted through faithful summarization.

After introducing himself, Al-Laqani states his purpose: he has summarized what his teacher (his father) annotated from the work 'Umdat al-Murid'. This reveals the text's nature as a filial and scholarly digest. Let's see what he says.

Guided Path
1
Read the purpose statement
Read the Arabic sentence stating the author's action: 'قد كنتُ لخصت...'
2
Understand 'ustādhunā'
The term 'our teacher' refers to his father, Ibrāhīm al-Laqānī. Recognize the humility even in referring to his father as a teacher.
This establishes the chain of transmission.
3
Identify the source works
The annotation is from 'Umdat al-Murīd', which is a commentary on the original poem 'Jawharat al-Tawḥīd'. Notice the relationship between the works.
'Umdat al-Murīd' is the commentary; the present work is a summary of that commentary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you approach learning as a mere consumer or as someone who faithfully transmits what you've received? What would change if you took every lesson as something to be summarized and passed on to others?
?
Your knowledge is built on the shoulders of teachers. Does your study reflect gratitude for their efforts, or do you rush ahead as if you invented the ideas yourself?
?
Al-Laqani preserved his father's exact creed. Is your own creed derived from reliable sources or from hearsay? What concrete step will you take today to verify the foundations of your faith?
